Title: Re: Pistilli
Post by: daisy on August 07, 2006, 11:42:39 AM
The one nice thing is that they do have an office where you can call and have your complaints/problems/issues heard regarding their rental units.  I lived in one of his blgs.  Some people I contacted at the office were very nice, others were not so nice.  It helps to be very informed of your rights as a tenant when calling, and written letters help as well.  Most of my issues were resolved, but it took multiple phone calls, and full knowledge of my rights.

That said, the blg I was in was rundown, very old, and not well-updated - one 15 watt of electricity for the entire two bedroom apt without grounded outlets - which meant you blew fuses on a regular basis.  The blg also had roach, mice and all sorts of other bug problems.  The last straw was the bed bug problem, which hastened my departure.   I was probably more vocal than most about the problem, but it did result in getting the entire blg, and all units granted access to, exterminated twice for bed bugs.  I could not beleive that people knew about this problem but most had chosen not to speak up about it, whether out of fear or indifference, I think it was both.   Unfortunately, if your neighbors do not let exterminators in, the pests accumulate and just come thru the walls to every other apartment.

When I moved in, the unit was negotiated lower based on my good credit.  Subsequently, every year the rent was raised to max rent stab laws allowed, and was non-negotiable.  Upon looking for apts with new realtors, I was told of his slumlord reputation, and quite a few said they did not work with him because renting to him reflected badly on their real estate company.   Be aware.

All I can say is good luck.  I have yet to hear of one positive experience with this landlord.

Title: Re: Pistilli
Post by: daisy on August 07, 2006, 01:01:44 PM
are you in 38th and Ditmars?  The exterminator there came once a month, and the super was really nice about you giving the key so they could exterminate if you weren't home.  Also had the schedule as well.  This website is a great resource for finding out your rights -http://www.housingnyc.com/html/resources/faq/quality.html#exterminate

I'd be very nervous about those bed bugs, be very careful.  They just came thru the walls and invaded everyone's apartments.  The vacuum is your best friend.  If you are getting bit, you may have them.

I remember his leases being very by the book, but don't recall anything about extermination in them.  However, they did send someone the very next day after my many numerous frantic phonecalls about the bed bugs.  I admit, I freaked out when I discovered them, but who in their right mind wouldn't?  I did not pay for any of the three visits to exterminate them.  Call the office, follow up, write letters, educate yourself of your rights.  They did fix problems although it took a long time.

Also did you put down a key deposit?  They are supposed to be illegal but I did get mine back upon moving out.  Also you are owed interest on your security when you move out, but most landlords pocket the money.  I managed to get this as well, through numerous phonecalls.

Title: Re: Pistilli Riverview East Apartments
Post by: BORNnRAISED on July 17, 2007, 10:10:27 PM
Did you get your loan through Pistilli's bank, First Central?  Because he is probably the only bank that would give a loan for that property.  Most banks require at least 50% of the units be sold first, some even 80%.  They can not even give you a straight answer when you ask them how many units are sold.  I keep getting "A couple" as a response.  Co-ops are a big risk for banks, and with Pistilli's name being on it, the risk is even greater.  The asking prices are through the roof, and the maintenance fees are also high, why??? Because he has until 2012 to payoff Eagle Electric, who still owns the land.  2012 is around the corner, what happens if he doesn't pay it off???  And besides, the quality of work in the units are so poor, you would have to buy it and renovate it right away.

Title: Pistilli Animal Neglect
Post by: Reader on August 01, 2007, 09:19:43 AM
There is a beautiful, friendly, 7-year-old female Rottweiler that Pistilli has been using to guard his Riverview Towers.  She lives (and has lived for a wicked long time now) alone in the garage.  She rarely sees the light of day, rarely goes outside and rarely has any company.

Some of the workers pay attention to her a little bit - they'll walk from time to time, play with her a little here and there when they can, but basically this poor dog lives confined and alone with no human or animal companions.

Somehow, she is still super sweet and friendly. 

I was told the building is set to 'open' 9/1...which means in a few weeks Pistilli needs to find someone to take the dog because she won't be "needed" anymore.

Apparently, they'll put an ad on Craigslist or something in an effort to find her a home.

If I could take this gorgeous girl myself I would, but we've already got a dog and you all know how hard it is to rent an apartment in Astoria if you have a dog, especially if it's a Rottweiler...

Let's hope this sweetie gets a lovely home with an owner who will love and care for her the way she deserves to be taken care of. 

Maybe one of you fits the bill... :-)

Title: Re: Pistilli Riverview East Apartments
Post by: Your Mother on September 09, 2007, 09:10:06 AM
I couldn't agree more here... I went to look at these places recently (mostly because of what I read on this board), and I can't see this place even getting off the ground.

1)  Nobody in their right mind would pay 800K for a large 2 bedroom in this place (especially being a co-op)... I suspect, if the seller is desperate, you could easily low-ball them 2-300K on the asking price since you'll have to put at least 100K into the larger units to actually fix them up (assuming the board even approves you to do this)
2)  The lofts are ridiculously laid out (The large martini glass pillars in the bedrooms are hilarious)
3)  The quality of the construction was terrible throughout the inside and outside of the building
-- we could see real evidence of water damage in certain units in the ceiling (I suspect the plumbing is very cheaply done, like the rest of the  building)
-- The vinyl flooring was already coming un-done
-- The kitchens were an absolute joke (the cabinets were already falling apart)
-- Any wood work done in the units looked like it was produced by a grade 9 shop class

For those that bought into this place, I feel for you... I really do.  The fact of the matter is that there are many large 1000 sqft+ units in this building that will probably never sell... I can't see people from the city buying into this place at all.

-Your Mother

Title: Re: Pistilli Riverview East Apartments
Post by: krocker on September 24, 2007, 12:28:18 PM
In my continuing, futile attempts to counter the negative hostility on this board, I would like to point out the positives of this development.   I feel that anyone browsing through the threads deserves a balanced picture of this place.  They have certainly heard plenty about the drawbacks.  Not everyone agrees the building is the hellhole most posters here make it out to be.   

Positives:

- Large square footage in apartments - much larger than anything else we looked at when shopping    around Astoria.

- Location, location, location:  Right on Astoria Park!

- 2/3 bedrooms have 2 bathrooms - and start at around $500k.  (I do agree that some of these units are overpriced for this neighborhood.  But not all.)

- Many have balconies

- Many have nice views - the better the view, the higher the $$ of course

- Bus stop directly in front of the building

- Doorman

- Exercise rooms

- Zoned for PS 122  - an excellent k-8 school with the middle school having a reputation as one of the best in the city

- Time Out NY named 19th street along the park one of the "top 50 blocks" to live on in the city.  The building is not on that exact block, but pretty darn close!

- Lots of good restaurants/shops within walking distance

- Nice neighbors:  we have met some of the others who have bought units in the building and everyone is very friendly, smart, nice, professionals - many have been in Astoria for a long time.   In our case, I am a television writer/producer and my husband is a chemical engineer.  We have been renting in Astoria for 7 years.

Title: Re: Pistilli Riverview East Apartments
Post by: jennifer on October 03, 2007, 05:36:00 AM
$500k does look like a bargain.  if you ignore the cheap quality of EVERYTHING.
 
However - the really expensive part is the $1/square foot maintenance.  So ... take the $500k unit - that's 1200 sq. ft (200 is outside on your balcony) and then you have to pay $1200/month in maintenance to upkeep a place that was poorly built in the first place. 

500k is no longer a bargain when you have to continue paying 1/2 of you current rent each month. 


AND the exercise rooms ... bah... don't get me started.  An alcove in a hallway  - where i'm sure the equipment is going to match the rest of the quality in the building.  Cheap! Not well done.
Title: Re: Pistilli Riverview East Apartments
Post by: Pulled pork on October 03, 2007, 06:30:15 AM
Went to see the place last month.  Its not that the appliances are cheap, its that the construction is cheap and you cant replace that.

-wood floors were put down like a laminate. Now all bubbled up and there were sagging dips in the floors of all 5 apartments we looked at.
-cracked marble molding everywhere.
-dark hallways
-exercise room turned out to be in front of two apartment entrances where 1 couch and two chairs should have gone.  can you imagine the odor and noise every time you walk past that spot?
-the interior apartments have giant windows facing opposite apartment windows or sitting areas.  People will be looking down at you at all times unless you put up black out curtains.
-the incredible maintenance costs.  You might as well by something by Gantry park for 70K more but with half the maintenance fees.

And the best part!  Our "salesman" was a young 20 something yutz who didnt wear a jacket, tried to make conversation by talking about an ex teacher of his that tried to fail him in high school and how he almost failed his regents exam. Then went on to explain that they will fix the shoddy construction when the place is finished by rehiring only the good construction guys to redo the apartmets.  When I asked why they didnt just do that know he mentioned that there is a contract to get the work done and "you know how t is" the crews just want to get each place done and get out, they dont care about doing a good job.  WOW what a salesman.  you would think that for 500k + they would have a qualified sales team in place.  You could have swapped these people with the hostess and bussing staff at Neptune diner.

Oh, we didn't buy a place there if you were wondering.
Title: Re: Pistilli Riverview East Apartments
Post by: krocker on October 03, 2007, 12:17:51 PM
OK, let me address some of these concerns:

- The building is new construction which means everything comes with a warranty.  Everything.  So if something goes wrong or breaks down, guess what?  They have to replace it.  And frankly these accusations of cheap construction have been blown way out of proportion.  It's not a completely false observation, but it's not as dire as some would have you believe.

- You have a final inspection as a buyer.  So if there's a crack in your molding or the floors are not up to snuff, guess what?  They have to fix that before you move in.  I am sure that's how they plan to handle any deficiencies that have developed in particular units.  They have a lot of apartments in that building, over 100 - it's difficult to keep up with all of them at the same time while trying to get the building open. And also remember how long these units have been up.  They've been moving at a snail's pace for years.  So that's years of exposure these units have had with no owners to keep up with them.

- Exercise rooms - whatever.  We were perfectly happy with what they showed us.  Picking on the exercise rooms is just being curmudgeonly.  I suppose you're looking for a five star resort gym and spa in your $300k one bedroom apt purchase?

- Costs - we've run our numbers with mortgage, maintenance, tax deductions, the whole bit.  We're satisfied that the costs are within our budget and is absolutely not out of line with other new coop developments.   Maintenance depends on the size of your unit.  If you can't afford a big place, well, that's not the building's fault.

- Bottom line - and obviously I can only speak for us personally -  the following were important to us in an apt:  spaciousness, location, the community within and around the building and a cost that fit our budget.  We have gotten everything we wanted on those points and then some with this purchase.  And while you continue to lob insults, just remember there are people in this neighborhood and on this board who are going to make this place their home soon.  You insult the development, you're insulting us by default.   If this place isn't for you for whatever reason, that's fine.  But I refuse to sit back and watch our future home and our decisions to purchase there get ripped to shreds any longer on this board.

Good to hear some more support for the building...

As someone looking to get a place in the apartments, I've read through all of the opinions and can understand the frustrations...one's home can be a very personal thing, and everyone has their opinions.  What it really comes down to is what is important for each of us in our idea of home.  For some, the emergence of a proportionately large condo building in a primarily 2 family house neighborhood is an unwelcome intrusion.  For me , it could also mean a shift in the character of the neighborhood.  Is it good or bad?  will each unit remake their space?  Will the co-op board change how the back exterior looks?  There's no way of telling for sure.  I'm excited to see what's possible.  The neighborhood has been changing, and I think that's a good for any community.  I'm all for hearing criticism, but I think some of the frustration I've felt has been from posts that are primarily speculation or uninformed.  We're all here saying something because it's important enough to us to express ourselves about it, and it's great that this has become a heated topic of discussion.. and I hope to hear a lot more about from everyone about it.

Title: Re: Pistilli Riverview East Apartments
Post by: Debbie on October 04, 2007, 08:59:46 PM
Folks - I am so excited that you are coming into the neighborhood.  I have been waiting for years for folks to finally move in!  You should know the following - our neighborhood is amazing for ten months of the year.  We feel like we are in the country - it is so peaceful.  The park is used by elderly, children with their parents, joggers, dog walkers etc. and most of us know each other!  From the beginning of July until Labor Day however, it is challenging.  The crowds come to the pool, it gets very dirty, it gets noisy, motorcycles race around the park etc.  We (some of the participants on this forum) are trying to organize a meeting with our local representatives to discuss some of Astoria's quality of life issues.  It would probably be quite educational for you to attend.  Look out for the date / time on Astorians.com.  Also, please do not let anything discourage you.  This is a wonderful place to live!

My wife and I took a tour of the facilities on Saturday.  Our sales agent was an idiot who couldn't actually answer any questions other than the square footage and price of each apartment he showed us.  I asked him if I could rip up the floor and put in new ones, since they were all laminate instead of real wood, and he couldn't answer me.

I took home their brochure.  I think this may have been mentioned already, but in their pitch about great restaurants, they have a photo of the Egyptian place on Steinway...not exactly a close walk.

Title: Re: Pistilli Riverview East Apartments
Post by: vinegartom on October 16, 2007, 01:23:43 PM
Having visited the co-op quite a few times, I can say it has both it's strengths and weaknesses...

To address the last few posts:

Sales staff: Some of them are not experienced at all.  Regarding the "putz" salesman, I think I know who you may be referring you to.  He's usually the person who comes to the lobby to escort prospective buyers to the sales office.  The saleswoman who originally showed us the units, Athena, was helpful and easy to talk to.
Realize, though, that most of the sales staff won't know the answers to questions about alterations and other specific things.  A good part of this is mainly because this is a new development that doesn't have any preexisting policy regarding rights/responsibilities/etc.  Many things may have not been determined yet...which gives all the tenants and buyers a strong negotiating position.  Bottom line is, the most effective person to talk to in the sales office is Kevin Riley, the Sales Director.  He's been able to answer pretty much every question we've had, and has always admitted to a "that hasn't been determined yet."  Although he usually doesn't show the units, he's the one you want to talk to first thing after seeing the units.

Kitchens: - Everyone is in agreement that the kitchens are small...the bigger question behind that, however, is if the kitchens are too small to be workable.  The unit I'm pursuing has a C - shaped kitchen, where the stove is opposite the dishwasher. At first I said," I won't be able to open the stove and the dishwasher at the same time!" - after which I wondered when I would ever in my entire life need to do that.  Historically, the kitchen has been gradually shrinking because it's function has changed so much in the last century.  I'm willing to bet that your kitchen is smaller than your parent's kitchen. If you're a professional chef, then yes, these kitchens will not be up to par at all.  But how much space do you actually need?  For some, they won't work.  For me (and I enjoy cooking) the c-shaped kitchen means that everything I need is no more than 2-3 steps away.  The fact that the cabinetry stops short of the ceiling will allow me to store books, bottles, and others things above.  So, for me, the kitchen works perfectly.  Also realize, by law, the appliances have to be above a certain standard to be approved - but why should the developers buy big fridges when a purchaser may most likely replace it with either one they already own or with one they really want. The same goes for the fixtures - most purchasers will also want to customize, so it doesn't make financial sense to focus your money there.

Brochure: Kevin has mentioned that the marketing needs an overhaul, and is cheesy.  I think we can all agree on that.

Moving in dates:  Although still an aggravation, the constant delays in finishing the building and having it ready are not uncommon.  A few pages back, someone posted a NYT article about how common this is with new condo/co-op developments in the past two years.. The best way to deal with this is establishing a drop-dead move-in date which can establish a responsibility on their part to have it ready, or suffer financial consequences.

the important thing is to ASK QUESTIONS.  If someone doesn't have the answer, ask for someone who will.  As a business transaction, you have the right to be fully informed.  Most places aren't trying to hide anything, as anything hidden will be revealed during an appraisal or when a real estate lawyer gets involved.  Be ruthless.  be a reporter.

I'm not sure what you mean by this. My kitchen is smaller than my parent's kitchen because I grew up in a house in the suburbs, and now I live in an apartment in the city. Maybe I'm weird, but whether I'm in an apartment or a house, I would like the kitchen to be bigger than the bathroom (and that doesn't mean I want the bathroom to be two feet wide, but the kitchen should be functional as a kitchen and not just a place to reheat food). I'd want enough counter space so that if I need to wash dishes in the sink or am preparing some food to be brought out to the dining room, I don't have to put plates on the stove in the meantime, or so I don't have a bunch of vegetables competing for the same foot of counter space when making a salad or sauce. I want enough cabinet space to store various dishes, including plates, bowls, pots, pans, mugs, and glasses. This especially goes if I'm going to spend hundreds of thousands of dollars on an apartment.

I am not a professional chef. I don't understand the implication that anyone with the money to buy a nice apartment won't ever want to cook a large meal.
Title: Re: Pistilli Riverview East Apartments
Post by: daisy on October 17, 2007, 07:17:41 AM
I have to think that the size of Manhattan kitchens have historically been shrinking over the years, and that the function of them has changed along with them.  This small kitchen "I eat out most of the time" mentality has started to translate into anything new being built not only in Manhattan but to Queens and Brooklyn as well.  Go to any of those new luxury McMansions being built all over upstate in the suburbs and you will see a huge kitchen that is in fact way bigger than my parents.   I do know a lot of peole who are just fine with a small kitchen.  And there are a lot of people who are not.  It's all a matter of preference, but the small kitchen is so prevalent in modern NYC construction that it makes it hard for people who enjoy cooking to be happy with buying a place here.  I'm curious to know if the developer could afford to invest in better appliances, fixtures, and construction based on what the place costs.  How much money just goes right to their pockets instead?  Ultimately, people want different things in buying their place, and I'm glad to hear it's working out for many of you.

Title: Re: Pistilli Riverview East Apartments
Post by: mikeyballoonz on November 03, 2007, 01:58:37 PM
What is EIFS?
EIFS stands for Exterior Insulation and Finish Systems. The product is also called synthetic stucco, and refers to a multi-layered exterior finish that's been used in European construction since shortly after World War II, when contractors found it to be a good repair choice for buildings damaged during the war. The majority of repairs to European buildings were to structures constructed of stone, concrete, brick, or other similar, durable materials.

EIFS in North America
North American builders began using EIFS in the 1980's, first in commercial buildings, then applying it as an exterior finish to residences--mostly wood frame houses--using the same techniques that had been successful in Europe.

Eagle Electric is a stone, brick and concrete structure. The problem with moisture comes with EIFS applied to wood frame homes.   :mrgreen:

Regarding all the comments regarding the building, whether this is considered a good "buy" or not:
If everyone thought the same way, everyone would make the same decisions and there would be a no arbitrage opportunity out there - in terms of an investment. So to the Buyers, Potential Buyers and to those that just post out-of boredom. Do your research, educate yourself, take a view, make a decision, stick to your decision and dont be concerned by anyone's comments. Title: Re: Pistilli Riverview East Apartments

NYMacUser - My parents purchased their house in 1973 in Astoria.  They were immigrants and blue collar workers.  I am the General Manager of a hotel.  I purchased my house in Astoria in 2000.

There is room for all of us.  A couple of Sundays ago, I passed by the large bank on the corner of 31st Street and Ditmars Blvd (Commercial?).  There were so many little children in there with their parents attending a Halloween Party.  The families are moving back, perhaps the parents are white collar workers, but what's wrong with that?  Should I have aspired to remain blue collar like my folks?  They made a decent living, worked hard and paid their dues, but they did that so that my siblings and I could do better.

Title: Re: Pistilli Riverview East Apartments
Post by: NYCMacUser on November 18, 2007, 12:53:00 AM
Quote from: Debbie on November 16, 2007, 09:10:07 PM
There is room for all of us.  <snip>  The families are moving back, perhaps the parents are white collar workers, but what's wrong with that?  Should I have aspired to remain blue collar like my folks?  They made a decent living, worked hard and paid their dues, but they did that so that my siblings and I could do better. 

Nothing stays as it was.  We may experience nostalgia for the old Astoria, as the new generation 30 years from now will pine for the Astoria of today.
I've taken my time to think and rethink how to respond to this. I am sorry, but I cannot agree with the premise. The rich and the poor cannot live in harmony. Not in these times. Astoria will now be a part of the nouveau riche experience because of the prices that are surrounding us. The rents, the property ownership, the restaurants, the small shops, all are slowly becoming too expensive for the average person.

Even many of the local supermarkets have changed to suit the needs of the these folks. One of them discontinued stocking dozens of food items to put in a whole isle cooler of assorted beers. Even the pasta isles have changed. Imported means higher prices and the average person can't afford any higher prices.

When postage-sized, studio apartments rent for $1600$1800, and people who obviously have the money rent them, everything goes up. When a one bedroom condo sells for $450,000$700,000 and people who can afford to pay that move into a community, they will be living their lifestyle and the neighborhood will be catering to them, and no longer concerned about the rest of the residents.

I was recently broadsided on 31st Street and Astoria Boulevard by a man driving a Lexus. When I asked him for his ID I saw that he lived in the luxury, Manhattan style and priced Gantry. He walked over to me while I was writing down his information and asked me if we could settle "it" right then and there because he was in a real rush to get to some kind of meeting. Then he reached into his pants pocket and pulled out a wad of hundred dollars bills. He started to peel them off and he handed me a dozen or so. He literally shoved them in my face like I was supposed to drool or something. I guess this is how you live when you have all that money to flaunt.

There are no luxury cars parked on MY block. People still greet each other by name. People who don't work know that they shouldn't use the laundry room on the weekends because that is the only time working folks have to do their laundry. I guess it's a matter of attitude. With all these luxury condos going up I fear that the flavor of Astoria will immeasurably change, and not for the betterment of the Community, but for the comfort and convenience of those luxury condo owners.

Astorians.com has a couple of threads dealing with where you come from. I wish someone would start a thread about where you plan on retiring and you will see that those luxury condo owners will be fleeing Astoria when the time comes and the community will be left in shambles.

I have 2 sons living in Los Angeles. They scoped out these condos the last time they were in town. They both agreed that they could get twice as much for the buck, at half the prices these places were asking.

I know things get more expensive all the time, but it sickens me to think that if North America has to revisit a downsizing/outsourcing period ever again that these buildings and their tenants will have to abandon our Community and it will be left without a waterfront for people to enjoy and boutique-like shops that will no longer be able to make the exorbitant rents and we will be so over-developed and so over-priced that Astoria will never be able to sustain itself as a middle-class, family oriented, affordable Community.

Of course, this is just my opinion, and at my age, it is fairly safe to assume that I will no longer be here to see it happen.

I see what you are saying, but I do think this is happening everywhere and not just in Astoria.  It happened first in all of Manhattan, and then anywhere close to Manhattan.  It happened in most of Brooklyn anywhere near the city and now it's happening in Queens, anywhere close to Manhattan.  It happens in Jersey City and Hoboken, Long Island, Westchester, and even as far away as Duchess County.  It's telling to see that anything new being built is either specifically luxury (more often) or specifically low-income (less often).  What about for us middle-income folk?  Those who live on 30K - 80K?  But I can't say it's correct to say that no one worked on Wall Street from Astoria before now, and I can't lay blame with those who buy luxury.  It's not that simple, and I don't have all the answers.  Developers ignore middle income people, and it's incredibly frustrating.
Title: Re: Pistilli Riverview East Apartments
Post by: Debbie on November 18, 2007, 01:57:35 PM
NYMacUser, you are pessimistic.  I learned a long time ago that I cannot expect things to remain as they are today.  When I visit my parent's hometown overseas, it is not the same place I visited 20 years ago.  I feel nostalgia for the old days, but I understand that change is a part of life.  I miss the old neighborhood, I miss my Aunts and Uncles who have passed, and I miss the simple way of life.  Even in this relatively small town on a relatively small island in the Meditteranean, life in the past 30 years has changed drastically.

Just to be clear, my husband and I have friends from all walks of life who sit on our porch.  Most live in the area.  We have a friend who is a pilot, a lady who works in a pita bread factory, two Grandmothers who look after their Grandchildren, our neighbors include two professional photographers, a CPA, an Attorney and other professionals.  We all get along, we all greet each other, we have great fun.  There is room for all of us - the professionals, the Grandmothers, the factory workers.

Also, the Astoria I grew up in (from 1973 to 1993) was Archie Bunker territory.  Sorry, but this is true.  Perhaps your area was better, but I could not wait to get away from the narrow minded, racsist folks who lived in my area.  Most are dead today and the people who have moved in tend to be far more open minded, well traveled, well read etc.  I am happy with some of the change, but I do not like the overcrowding, expensive rents, no place to park etc.  I think we should work on solving the quality of life issues so that we can all live together in Astoria.

I could care less about what people buy with the money they earn -- but I do care when they treat the properties they buy in Astoria strictly as commodities, as disposable, i.e, simply as instruments to be exploited or bought and sold on a whim, without concern to or consideration of the value of the community and the people who live here.

Quote from: alia on November 18, 2007, 05:16:37 PM
not to get philosophical, but I think people who can buy their way, value other people less because they don't realize that they need (or might need) other people in the future.

I have to agree with this and add a little more to it in that I think it's becoming more and more common in society.  I worked as a firefighter in Texas before moving up here (so that I can also give myself the best shot with FDNY) and noticed this more than I thought I would.  When tax levies come around that include increased spending for fire departments (equipment, raises, new stations/not closing old ones, etc.), people tended to moan and groan and talk about "we don't have many fires/don't they get paid enough to sit around the station?/isn't that truck only 10 years old/why do they need new AEDs?"

However, as soon as it was their house on fire, their child not breathing, or their father having a heart attack, we couldn't get there fast enough.  People seem to think only of themselves and their own situations.  If it doesn't pertain to them, they don't care. 

I also notice this whenever an emergency vehicle is responding to a call; both when I was driving and when I see them here in the city.  Cars don't get out of the way, people keep crossing the street, and the trucks can't move.  What if that unit was responding to a 911 call at their house, or to a member of their family, or to a friends?  Wouldn't they be upset to know the unit was going to help someone they know and see that some a-hole was blocking the way and not caring that the people coming to help can't get through?

Sorry to get off on a rant (and WAY off the topic of this post), but that statement touched off aggravation and reminded me how much we have degraded as a society when it comes to caring about strangers around you and attempting to understand their situation by putting yourself in their shoes.

Title: Re: Pistilli Riverview East Apartments
Post by: NYCMacUser on November 18, 2007, 10:59:10 PM
I believe that the next World War is not going to be racially or gender based, or another Ethnic Cleansing or even a Jihad. I believe it is going to be the poor against the rich; the have-nots fending off their own decimation from the haves. I believe that everyone will have to take a side because I believe that all human-kind will fall into one or the other category. There will be no middle class to be indecisive. What used to be charming Communities like Astoria will be the new South Bronx of yesteryear. Rotting and abandoned by the wealthy who have gone on to do the same to other nice Communities while the poor are left in their dust to try to salvage their lives.

Astoria always had (so-called) white collar people here. We always had 6-figure earners among us. But they didn't flaunt their wealth and expect the Community to change for them. If you wanted a 4 star meal, you went to Manhattan. You didn't expect the restaurant to open up in your neighborhood. If you wanted the front of your building kept clean, you spoke to your landlord or picked up a broom and swept it clean yourself. You didn't expect the Community to devise some funding to make it cleaner for you. If you got sick, your neighbors looked in on you and helped you out. You weren't left to die alone at home because your neighbors didn't even know who you were. If your laundry got lost by the laundromat's "laundry lady" the owner expected and got a reasonable list of the costs of the items that were lost and offered you either the cash or a free year of bi-weekly laundering. If someone was lurking around your car, a neighbor called you and the police to be sure you didn't suffer any damage. If you were standing in front of your house, a neighbor driving by would ask you were you were going and offer to drive you to the doctor 15 blocks away or to the hospital to visit your Mom in Manhattan. If the grocery bags you were schlepping seemed too heavy, someone would offer to carry them home for you. Astoria had so many local saloons where Mom and Pop could go on a Saturday night for a couple of beers just to get out of the house. If you wanted to dance or otherwise drink and do something else, you went to Manhattan. You didn't expect it to be happening around the corner from where you lived.

I find the changes dehumanizing and extravagant. Hmmmm, perhaps I should purchase a fiddle?

Title: Re: Pistilli Riverview East Apartments
Post by: 28Grand on December 06, 2007, 09:26:27 AM
I re-read through this thread again. The last time I did so I had second thoughts about some of the comments I posted, thinking them too harsh especially about the aesthetics of Riverview East.

It didn't fully register with me that the apartments were finally on the market, people were actually buying them and that we'll soon have some new neighbors so I should have been, and be, more neighborly.

To the new owners: You are rightly upset about the delay in taking possession of your apartments and I hope all is resolved soon.

But I would like you also to consider about something: do you think you're the only people who have a stake in what happens to this building?

A bit of history: When the Met apartment complex on Ditmars went co-op in the 80s and became the Acropolis everyone when rah! rah! and hopped on the buying-mania band wagon

Unfortunately the stock market crashed in 86, the real estate market cooled, and the Acropolis almost ended up in receivership which would have resulted in everyone losing their shirts.

Were it not for Queens Borough President Claire Shulman, who stepped up and negotiated a deal to save the complex everyone would have lost I mean everyone: existing rental tenants, the new individual owners and the Ditmars community, who would have had to live with a 618-unit building cast adrift without any real management.

http://query.nytimes.com/gst/fullpage.html?res=990CEEDA1E3FF935A15750C0A963958260&sec=&spon=&pagewanted=all (http://query.nytimes.com/gst/fullpage.html?res=990CEEDA1E3FF935A15750C0A963958260&sec=&spon=&pagewanted=all)

http://cooperator.com/articles/248/1/New-Lending-Options-for-Co-ops/Page1.html (http://cooperator.com/articles/248/1/New-Lending-Options-for-Co-ops/Page1.html)

Now here we are again. I'm sorry but two, rather large apartment complexes by the same developer are entering on the cusp of a downward market (Riverview East now and Stern's warehouse, hopefully soon) with what seems like very poor management everyone should be concerned.

Perhaps the negativity in some of the comments stems from the fact that both buildings have been under construction for what seems like forever. I think it took less time to build the Empire State Building then it has to complete Riverview East and Sterns which to me never seemed quite right or bode well for future residents. Is it so surprising that it also seems to be taking as long to gain possession of your apartments?

Regardless of how I feel though, the complexes are built and it's to the benefit of the entire community that all the apartments are sold and occupied. It would be far worse for the buildings to remain half sold and potentially half empty.

I'm very sorry for any difficulty any buyers are finding themselves. I'm aware it's your money on the line, but what happens in that building will affect everyone in and around it.

Title: Re: Pistilli Riverview East Apartments
Post by: pdv2101 on December 06, 2007, 05:11:20 PM
To: Handyman

First of all if your intention is to be helpful, could you please post some information about your credentials/background (i.e. age, education, work experience, etc). I think everyone would like to know a little bit more about a person that makes a blanket-statement like this.

The question you have raised is valid, but it's something that even a ten year old would have thought of. There could be many answers to that question. It is primarily dependant on the type of boiler they have installed and the buildings HVAC duct architecture/layout. I have an engineering degree (EE) as an undergrad and worked for a consulting engineering firm for a couple of years in the City - before I went back to school and changed careers. Even though I was not a mechanical engineer, this is what I recall:

Large developments, such as this building could be designed to isolate certain sections or even control the air flow to designated areas. You can even have a duct system that can provide independent air-flow to every unit (you dont need a boiler per apt). Usually, the main ducts are in the corridors and branch out into the units via a 'gate' that controls the air-flow. I dont know the specs of the installed boiler nor have I seen the duct layout, so I cannot make the statement that the boiler can operate on an approx. 25% capacity (25% occupancy ~47/188) or if the air-flow can be controlled to satisfy the initial (Phase-I) populated sections of the building. However, these are very common practices so I expect something to that extent, especially for the boiler.

I asked Kevin regarding the heating issue a couple of months ago, when they started using the electric heaters in the sales office. I asked, how come they were not using the buildings heating units and the answer was that there "has" to be a minimum of units occupied to in order to operate the buildings boiler. I guess the cost of operating the boiler to its minimum capacity far exceeded the cost of the electric heaters.

Question: "Handy-man" does the "Handy" refer to something specific?

Next time you decide to post and raise a question (that might worry some people for no reason), it would be great if it could be accompanied with a list of possible answers or even sources (like "28Grand" did - Thanks for those article by the way, very informative - worth while reading) that support your argument and serve to clarify certain concerns...be informative or even be helpful aka "handy"
otherwise you only cause static on this forum

Title: Re: Pistilli Riverview East Apartments
Post by: Debbie on December 08, 2007, 08:00:26 AM
To everyone who purchased in the building - congratulations!!! I am delighted for you and look forward to having you as neighbors.  I was sick of looking at the old run down factory and although it is not the most attractive building now, it sure beats the overgrown brush and dilapidated walls.  Please include me in any meetings / events.  Although I do not live in the building, I am very active in the community and very familiar with all the quality of life issues that focus specifically in our area (namely, the issues that come with living across the street from the park).  Besides, I want to meet all of you!

Secondly, I think the Astorians on this board should welcome everyone and not be so negative and so mean spirited.  For goodness sakes, is that how you welcome people to our area???  How rude and unfriendly!!!  If my neighbors came over when I purchased my house and said - 'oh, look at your yard, it is so unruly' or 'you know, you'll have problems with your electricity' or 'you may get floods in your basement'...  well, guess what, there are issues with every house / apartment purchase.  My husband and I have spent eight years getting this house to where we want it to be.  It has been costly, difficult (we had to move out for a week last year to get our bathroom and other renovations completed) and emotionally draining at times, but it is what comes with making an investment in your future.

For those of you who are moving in, a heartfelt welcome.  I look forward to meeting you.  And Latinguy, come over any time. 

Regards,

DR

Title: Re: Pistilli Riverview East Apartments
Post by: Gleason on December 21, 2007, 08:18:51 AM
We are drifting off target here (my personal fav would have been to make his buildings artist lofts/cooperatives, but something like that requires a tincture of imagination and creativity in a community whose leadership is running on autopilot)

Take a look at the areas that are considered attractive - a nice mix without a five story monster three feet from your bedroom window and sunlight and quiet in a backyard, not some noisy human warehouse that seems to be the style in our community these days.

Perhaps taxes can even be used, instead of helping developers, ensure we all get a steady supply of electricity, school desks for the kids, new hospitals, firehouses, more police officers, more garbage pickups - we all know the routine by now.

Areas like downtown Flushing, or Elmhurst, are terribly overcrowded, and are not considered good places - even the immigrants that live there want to leave ASAP. They are a cautionary tale for the tear down and build set.

Again, those that like development, like those that want to tear down Old Astoria and are against landmarking, are a small radical but very vocal element of our community. To date they refuse to share the nuggets of their 'wisdom' outside of our community -and until they do so - perhaps we should just all tune them out. They seem to be out of step with the rest of the city.

As to real estate prices climbing, you have infinite demand on a very finite supply. We will never get ahead of this problem as long as our country has no borders. It will get worse. A lot worse.

Now back to Pistilli.
Title: Re: Pistilli Riverview East Apartments
Post by: Harlan on December 21, 2007, 09:56:57 AM
Look, I am for improvement of neighborhoods, not merely increasing density with shoddy construction. Improvement means just that. More people get to live in a nicer neighborhood.

Incidentally, I fully agree with you Gleason, that artist lofts/co-ops would have been preferable to the condos...

A few other points... Astoria is geographically close to the largest economic engine in the US, making it an ideal place for people who work in the city to live. This is a neighborhood that should be denser, because it is close to good public transportation. I'm very opposed to suburban sprawl for the same reason, that it's far from public transportation. (People have to live somewhere... Should it be farther out on Long Island?)

The reason politicians support development is exactly taxes. The Pistilli building will bring in hundreds of thousands of dollars a year to the city that the abandanoned factory would not. How do you propose to pay for all the police, hospitals, schools, etc, without money coming in to the city? Raising property taxes on the existing property owners even higher? Seems like a bad idea to me, but maybe you think that's the solution...

Flushing and Elmhurst certainly aren't ideal neighborhoods, but the problem isn't density. They're nowhere near as dense as, say, the UWS or the West Village, which are considered great places to live.

Quote from: Gleason on December 21, 2007, 08:18:51 AM
As to real estate prices climbing, you have infinite demand on a very finite supply. We will never get ahead of this problem as long as our country has no borders. It will get worse. A lot worse.

You should support Pistilli, then, since it's rich yuppies moving there instead of brown people...

There's certainly a supply-demand issue in NYC, but the problem isn't going to be solved until either the amount of housing increases, ideally in areas well-served by public transportation, or until people stop wanting to live and work here. And I'd hate for the latter to happen, wouldn't you?

Title: Re: Pistilli Riverview East Apartments
Post by: Frappe on December 21, 2007, 11:21:05 AM
The 26,751 p/sqm population density quote is for all of New York City, not for New York County.  Manhattan has about 1.5 million people living in 23 square miles, which makes a population density of 66,000 p/sqm.  Mind you, those 23 square miles includes governors island, wards/randals island, ellis island, central park, the docks and the bulkhead line and the business districts which are comparatively empty of residential development.

When you consider the "hot neighborhoods" of the UES, UWS and the Village, you are easily approaching if not surpassing densities of 100,000 p/sqm.

As for the Astoria population density quote, it does not seem correct.  It states a population of 43,151 people and a population density of 50,455 p/sqm.  That would breakdown to a square mileage of .85 square miles.  This indicates to me, in particularly because Astoria has a population approaching 140,000 and much larger than .85 square miles, that the 50,455 p/sqm number is the most dense part of Astoria and does not reflect the neighborhood on the whole.  It is most likely about only 50% to 60% of the stated number.

Note:  This is merely a point of clarification.  Its an interesting debate which I am enjoying just listening to and I have not made up my mind about which way I am leaning.

It's not like all that exists is one- and two-family houses on leafy, lawned side streets. We have quite a varied housing stock, including both small and large apartment buildings and we have room for more, including bigger structures -- but in my opinion development should take into consideration the types of buildings that surround it and also in my opinion, don't we deserve something more classy, for lack of a better word, or at least more designed than what's currently being built?

Only fools count on a rising property market and believe what is popular today will remain so tomorrow.


Gertting back to the subject of this thread, the Pistilli properties:

There was a great opportunity to transform both the Eagle Electric plant and Sterns warehouse, two landmark, historic properties into something special. They would of course be out of scale to the rest of the neigborhood but in the hands of the right developers the buildings could have been transformed into great looking, taxes generating properties that would be an asset to the neighborhood.

My apologies to the new owners at Eagle, I don't mean to offend but in my opinion what we now have is a big disappointment -- at least looking at it from the outside. My hope is that once people move it the building will be transformed somewhat and become more alive -- fingers crossed! -- and a sincere good luck to those moving in. We need you now.

In the case of Sterns warehouse there was a strong sentiment among residents living around it that the building should be pulled down and replaced with row houses. To me this would have been worse than what we have now (but not by much).



Title: Re: Pistilli Riverview East Apartments
Post by: SeminoleB on December 22, 2007, 12:38:39 PM
Quote from: Mr. Oliver on December 22, 2007, 12:00:00 AM
Man, that's an ignorant statement.  New York City real estate can go down in value, and has gone down in value.  Look into what happened in the condo bust of the 80s.

New York City may be coming late to the declining real estate party, but there is room for everyone.

Oh, and NYC will be there, if fashionably late.  There are (at least) three reasons that it hasn't hit the skids yet.

1. The average foreclosure time in New York State is about 445 days, which puts it at #1 on the list of longest foreclosure times.  This is about 3x (or more) longer than most of the other states that you're hearing so much about now (CA, OH, MI, FL, etc.) and it is a full 145 days longer than the 2nd longest.

Because of this, NYS is usually much later to the party and stays much longer.

Here's my source:http://www.mortgage-investments.com/borrow-money/foreclosure-laws.htm (http://www.mortgage-investments.com/borrow-money/foreclosure-laws.htm)

2.  The dollar is weak compared to the other world currencies for a multitude of reasons.  This is bringing investors from other countries to scoop up real estate "deals" here in the city, as they are now at a 20-40% discount to them because the currency conversion rate is so skewed in their favor.  Remember when the Euro was equal to the Dollar, or when you could get $1.50 Canadian dollars for $1 US?  Not anymore.  It's a little under $1.50 for 1 Euro and the CDN $ is about equal (at one point, it was worth MORE) than our dollar.  So, someone that had 200,000 Euros now has about 300,000 in US dollar buying power.

Thank you, Federal Reserve, for printing money at will, which has flooded the market with dollar bills and driven their value down in relation to the world market, which takes money out of each American's pocket (those imported goods now cost more to buy).

But, I digress...

3.  Wall Street bonuses were not terrible across the board this year, but they'll very likely be down next year.  The financial industry is probably going to take a big hit in 2008, which will bring laid off people and fewer/smaller bonuses.  This will hurt all of those new developments and bring up foreclosures, bigtime.

While buoyed temporarily by decent Wall Street bonuses this year and the influx of foreign cash, the NYC market will very, very, very likely come down before too long.  Nothing is immune.

Title: Re: Pistilli Riverview East Apartments
Post by: Bentleys Dad on December 24, 2007, 10:55:34 AM
Quote from: matty3bucks on December 24, 2007, 10:34:49 AM
What percentage of the market did this condo bust actually affect, seriously?  I can assume you are forgetting about the co-op conversion successes that happened when this "condo bust" also happened.

Were you even around when this happened, matty? I remember it well. The boom and bust of the 1980's and early 1990's was created by a confluence of factors. Changes in the tax code spurred a speculative building boom in New York, which led to a glut of apartments on the market. Then, in October 1987, the stock market collapsed, rocking consumer confidence and wounding the city's economic base. Finally, the crisis in the savings and loan industry led to a tightening of lending requirements that greatly restricted the availability of loans. But it's a different city now, and the fact that crime is lower makes a difference. Plus there are far more foreign investors buying here than ever before.
  But, to say that Astoria will never have a "low demand " for housing, just because it is "20 minutes from Manhattan" is utterly ridiculous. It has happened before and it is happening again. Look at what's  happening in Miami, which up until a few months ago had a booming condo market. Well, ok the builders might think that the market is still booming, but it is definitely a buyers market, as people are unable to offload what they bought thinking it was a good deal at the time. Do some research next time.

Title: Re: Pistilli Riverview East Apartments
Post by: pdv2101 on February 26, 2008, 08:12:29 AM
When you criticize the aesthetics of the Eagle building , in essence you are judging all of Astoria - correct? I personally can't differentiate the two. The only reason that the Pistilli developments are so utterly unattractive is to fit in, both aesthetically and architecturally, with the rest of Astoria. Could someone elaborate what your benchmark is when you judge a building on how it looks? Please give me an address and so I can drive by there Please dont even mention Astoria Towers, a 20-25 year old building with their 6' ceiling apartments that has the feeling of a grave and that go for 900$/sq-ft.
I've been living in Astoria for the past 6 years and I would describe the average house in Astoria as a semi-attached, wooden-framed, 2 family house that is approx 50-60 years old, which is literally rotting and slowly falling apart. The main thing that Astoria has to offer to newcomers is its geographical location. Astoria is so densely populated, not for its great "neighborhood" nor its beautiful buildings, but because of the convenient commute to the city and its ethnic diversity. I've had many friends from Europe visit Astoria.people with different backgrounds, ethnicities and social status (white, blue, red, yellow and purple collar people) and they all had the same opinion - that they were visiting a third world country. Thank God the City was close, so they left with a good impression at the end. You don't need to travel that far to become objective with what is beautiful. You just need to cross the Atlantic ocean - that's all. But then again - I don't think any of us has chosen to live in Astoria because of its beauty - we all have our personal reasons.
Very few buildings and neighborhoods get a passing grade in Astoria. Perhaps, 19th street is one of those and that's only because of the "park," which acts as some sort of an asylum and refuge from the rest of Astoria. However, any brand new development/conversion gets some sort of extra credit in my opinion, which makes it at least above the average.
Before people start commenting on how ugly any building is in Astoria, first take a look around you and then recalibrate your measurements of judgment...before this forum really starts to get ridiculous. Where do you live?
I take the Triboro every dayand my observation is a little more broader than yours Handyman, I look at Astoria as a wholeit reminds me of some documentaries they show for Kazakhstan....I can see the cruise ship metaphor, but I also see hundred of other smaller ships buried - a grave-yard of buried ships!
If there was a unit of repulsiveness, per unit the Eagle building is equally ugly to any other in Astoria. It's just much bigger, so it seems uglier as it sticks out to the human eye

Title: Pistilli Grand Manor on Ditmars
Post by: Publicolas on February 28, 2008, 10:46:58 AM
Hello, I am new to this board and wanted to introduce myself.  I have been renting in Astoria (really upper Ditmars) for the past 2 1/2 years and am currently in the market to buy something.  However, because I seem to work all of the time, I really am not that interested in buying a single family home and be saddled with the upkeep and other headaches that come with it (for that matter, a 2-family with the prospect of a renter seems even more daunting). As such, I would prefer to buy in a new development somewhere in Astoria that will require little, if any, work at the outset.  Specifically, I was wondering if people on this board have been to the open houses at the Pistilli Grand Manor on Ditmars (I think the cross street is 45th st).  I have visited and have some mixed feelings.  I really like that area of Astoria and the square footage in the condos is great, but I'm concerned about the (very) high monthly maintenance fees.  My overall first impression, however, was favorable (if you can get over the combined costs of maintenance and parking along with a mortgage, which I don't know if I can yet). 

I was wonder if others on this board have taken a look at the apartments and, if so, what their general impressions were?  Secondly, I was wondering if others on the board have "heard" anything negative (or positive for that matter) about this building? 

I was hoping this post could serve as a new thread on the topic of the Grand Manor.

Thanks.
Title: Re: Pistilli Grand Manor on Ditmars
Post by: flikchik on March 04, 2008, 11:07:14 AM
I went to the Grand Manor open house on Saturday 3/1. 
on the plus side, I too like the area, the rooms are HUGE and your maintenance covers the central heat & a/c, doorman and porter.

Now, the minus side.  The material used looks cheap and the fridge and stove are bottom on the line. Prices are based on square footage, so because the room are so big, most 1 bedrooms are around $380,000 and maintenance is around $800 per month (again, depending on the square footage.)   Also, there are parking spots but those are an additional $185 per month.

All in all....the prices are much too expensive for what you're getting.

Hope that helps, and good luck.

Title: Re: Pistilli Grand Manor Condominiums
Post by: thecatsmeow on March 19, 2008, 11:47:08 PM
I was at the open house.  I'm not an expert on kitchens and appliances, but there are nice, modern, stainless steel appliances and cabinets...and there's what's in the ditmars condo -  they're the kind that you DEAL WITH when you're a college kid getting you're first apartment.  The stove was the kind from 1970 with no clock.  You can find modern stuff in little studio apartments around here for that matter.  I mean, this is an investment of hundreds of thousands of dollars, in a brand new building that's immaculate on the outside, and the inside doesn't match the splendor...AT ALL.  Especially for their abnormally high asking price and maintenance, you should get the best.  As far as upper ditmars, all the luxury condos seem to be going up in L.I.C.  We looked at one on the water where the appliances, the cabinets, the wall sockets, bathroom fixtures were what you'd find in a luxury hotel.  And of course, I'm not suggesting Brooklyn when you're talking about upper ditmars, but my point is, there's a new condo there with amazing bathrooms, a huge kitchen island...outstanding.
They've been building that thing for a long time, and there was a big anticipation.  I've heard from 5 or 6 people who went to the open house and they were disappointed.  I've heard people who heard people were disappointed.  Even the people at he walk-thru appeared un-impressed.  It even seemed that the layout in some of the bigger condos had the odder kitchen layout, very narrow where the door of the fridge nearly hits the oven across from it - not the type of awkwardness you expect from a condo.  But at the end of the day, you have to formulate an opinion by seeing the place doing what makes you happy.

I agree with this statement.  The new condos around Queens Plaza and along the LIC waterfront are all 650k and up for 2 bedrooms.  Pistilli is asking much lower.  Are the finishes cheap?  Maybe.  And I'm not defending the developer for cheaping out.  But the fact is, at that price - just trash the appliances and do a "light" kitchen renovation before moving in.  It sounds nuts but the fact is, you're still way ahead of the game.  You want deluxe finishes and amenities, then get ready to pay a much higher price.  You can get a top-of-the-line range, refrigerator and dishwasher for about $6000 for the three.  Not a lot of money in the whole scheme of things.  Besides, I'd be much more concerned why they don't have a 421a tax abatement in place.  If they blew it - then those common charges are out of sight.

Title: Re: Pistilli Grand Manor Condominiums

I went for a viewing on these condos. They weren't bad. I agree that the kitchens do not have the best appliances, but that's something that can be easily changed. The maintenance at the Acropolis is just a little cheaper for a way smaller apartment with less amenities.

This neighborhood (Ditmars area) is really tough to buy in. It's either a co-op or condo for around 300k (and up) or to buy a house for a minimum of 700k (and up). There is not a lot of selection.

Title: Re: Pistilli Grand Manor Condominiums
Post by: TJT on March 21, 2008, 10:19:29 AM
You know, I really wish more builders would offer buyers a choice of finishes.  What I mean is they price the raw space at a certain amount.  Say $300,000.  Then offer the buyer plan A, B, or C.  Plan A might be the economy finishes (floors, cabinets, appliances, fixtures) and would add maybe $20,000.  And Plan C would be the high end like the Sub Zero, the Viking Range, etc and maybe add $50,000.  I realize it can be labor intensive and can create some confusion.  But the builder usually orders and pays in bulk for all three categories and can pretty much quickly transform each unit accordingly.  Then the buyer gets what they like and what they can afford.  This is very common in suburban housing developments.  I think the reason Pistilli tends to offend a lot of people is that he seems to push out the lowest quality possible in order to make the highest profit.  So the happiness of the buyer is not even part of the equation.  That's not a very nice feeling for a new homeowner to have.

Title: Re: Pistilli Riverview East Apartments
Post by: Tati on March 24, 2008, 12:25:12 AM
Quote from: Debbie on February 24, 2008, 07:15:02 AM
Why don't you enlighten us?

Hey guys, sorry for the delay on replying to this but just in case you were still left wondering... this information is available in the bldg's offering plan which you receive once you are looking into buying into it... my fiancee and I almost bought an apt there, but we decided it wasn't the right place for us at the time. It's quite a risky buy, not because of the 22 mil debt, whic now is about 18 mil. First of all, the building owes this much, not Pistilli himself... The reason why is because the Coop (which is in fact a corporation) took an underlying mortgage of 20 something Mil when it was first created to build the building. This cost is included in the monthly maintnance payments, which explains why maintnance is so so HIGH!!! The way they catch you is because they say MAYBE, in 3 years, the building MIGHT become a Condo, IF the Board votes for it... yada yada, then they say the value MIGHT go up... oh I was almost forgeting, this building does NOT own the land where it stands, therefore, they also tell you IF the bldg becomes a Coop, the Sponsor MIGHT purchase the land and he MIGHT give the Coop its right to the land... The bottom line, I don't think you should buy into it unless you know for a fact that you have the stomack to go through this transition in 3 years or so, and  you should probably wait that long, otherwise, I don't think it would be worth the investment. But if you are in it for the long term, I think you have good chances of making a good deal... we did not go for it cause we are a young couple considering moving away, so it just wasn't the right place for us, but wish everyone luck who are considering it. I just think you should negotiate really well because it seems they were having a tough time selling it, not sure that's still the case today. We looked back in Jan'08.

Title: Re: Pistilli Riverview East Apartments
Post by: TJT on March 24, 2008, 01:47:51 PM
Quote from: daisy on March 24, 2008, 01:19:59 PM
It could be that buyers bought the units as an investment opportunity and are now renting out the unit they bought, instead of moving in themselves.  Or that Pistilli is having trouble finding buyers, and is now renting units to make up for it. 

It's most likely the latter.  If this were a condo, then renting by owners would be permissible.  But being a coop, it's highly unlikely that a brand new owner can rent the unit from day 1.  There must be some kind of bylaws in place.  It's more likely that sales are sluggish and they're renting just to fill up the building and not have such a tremendous loss.  But none of this spells good news for this building.  That is from an investment position.  As many have predicted, this seems to have been a failed venture.  With luck and perseverance, the owners who did committ to this building will be able to form a board and somehow turn this into a viable and profitable situation.  Lots of buildings have a rough start.  But in time it can completely turn around.  What's sad is that it was all so predictable.  The poor construction.  Taking more time to build than the new Citifield (Shea).  The lousy marketing.  The shameful website.  Absolutely nothing was done right, it seems.

Title: Re: Pistilli Riverview East Apartments
Post by: TJT on March 24, 2008, 03:26:37 PM
Quote from: latinguy72 on March 24, 2008, 01:56:48 PM
Just an FYI, the developer from day one was going to sell a certain percentage of the units and rent a certain percentage. As time goes by, renters will have the option of buying if they choose. Also, in all fairness it is way too soon to state that this development has been a failure.

I mean no insult, so for that, I apologize.  But I don't think it's too soon if you look at the facts and if you, like many of us, saw this building sit forever in a transition mode.  And now witness all these starts and stops.  Almost a month without TV/internet is not at all commonplace in any new construction at the luxury level.  It's nothing short of astonishing.  But nothing with this development seems shocking anymore.  And I realize how elitist this sounds - but in an owner occupied building, you do not want to have any kind of substantial population of renters.  It means a financial disaster down the road.  And serious trouble with prospective buyers getting mortgages.  But to be clear, I am NOT mocking renters.  If this whole building had become a luxury rental, then all would be well and good.  But this is a coop that failed to sell out the property and has turned to renting a large portion.  That's not good.  This is why many coops forbid and/or strictly limit subletting.  And many condos put caps on units that are not owner occupied.  The thinking is that renters are more transient and don't have a vested interest in the property.  Hence, they will not make improvements to their apartments and raise the property value.  And furthermore, may not be concerned about bettering the common areas.  In today's economy, it is not uncommon for a developer/sponsor to turn to renting some of the last few remaining units.  This is happening now at Arris Lofts.  But for Pistilli to plan to sell some and rent others from the start is very unorthodox.  But nothing about this building has followed any general principles of a standard luxury conversion.

Title: Re: Pistilli Riverview East Apartments
Post by: krocker on March 24, 2008, 11:45:48 PM

Quote from: TJT on March 24, 2008, 03:26:37 PM
I mean no insult, so for that, I apologize.  But I don't think it's too soon if you look at the facts and if you, like many of us, saw this building sit forever in a transition mode.  And now witness all these starts and stops.  Almost a month without TV/internet is not at all commonplace in any new construction at the luxury level.  It's nothing short of astonishing.  But nothing with this development seems shocking anymore.  And I realize how elitist this sounds - but in an owner occupied building, you do not want to have any kind of substantial population of renters.  It means a financial disaster down the road.  And serious trouble with prospective buyers getting mortgages.  But to be clear, I am NOT mocking renters.  If this whole building had become a luxury rental, then all would be well and good.  But this is a coop that failed to sell out the property and has turned to renting a large portion.  That's not good.  This is why many coops forbid and/or strictly limit subletting.  And many condos put caps on units that are not owner occupied.  The thinking is that renters are more transient and don't have a vested interest in the property.  Hence, they will not make improvements to their apartments and raise the property value.  And furthermore, may not be concerned about bettering the common areas.  In today's economy, it is not uncommon for a developer/sponsor to turn to renting some of the last few remaining units.  This is happening now at Arris Lofts.  But for Pistilli to plan to sell some and rent others from the start is very unorthodox.  But nothing about this building has followed any general principles of a standard luxury conversion.

This is full of errors.  One thing I can't stand is when people post information as fact when they do not know the real story.  And that has been the case for so long with this particular building.

Yes, they are renting - but they only have a certain percentage set aside that they will rent.  The percentage is designed so we can have a coop board and we will be running the building. And the percentage is also set so future buyers will have no trouble getting mortgages.  They had planned to rent all along.  I'm sure if they had sold out immediately, they wouldn't have rented, but it's fine by me if they exercise their right to.  I don't mind renters as long as they are discriminating about who they're renting to, and they seem to be doing that.

Also, sales are doing better now that people are living in the building.  Still slow, but with the market we're in and the neverending slams this development has received, that's not too surprising.

As for developments having trouble/delayed openings/issues that need fixing when it first opens, this is more normal than not.  There was a big article in the NY Times last weekend about an expensive coop building in Manhattan that had all kinds of problems leading up to and after opening.  But now, a few years down the road, they are doing fine.

Lastly, this is NOT a luxury coop building.  You want luxury, go look in LIC.  These are spacious apartments priced reasonably for their location and size.  If they were luxury and selling at luxury prices, most of us buyers wouldn't be living there.

As for reports from the inside, our unit and the others that I've visited (that belong to really nice, friendly neighbors) are just lovely.  Big, open, nice views, fantastic super who's really responsive to any problems/issues. 

And as for predictions of the building failing - well, there were plenty of predictions that the building would never open, too.  And yet here we are.

I haven't logged on in a while...I just want to check on how everyone who bought/rented is doing.  We love being in the building despite "growing pains" the building is going through.  We bought our unit knowing we had to do renovations.  I've read in other threads that this shouldn't have to be the case.  Honestly I wish we don't have to and just move into a perfect apartment.  The reality is the "perfect ones" that we've looked at are usually unaffordable (> 600K).  It's been a real sacrifice these past couple of months going through renovating our living room and kitchen (mostly the kitchen).  Our living room is finally done for the most part...the kitchen should be done next Saturday.  Over the next months we will be doing smaller renovations.

Here's how the living room looked before:

(http://img168.imageshack.us/img168/1662/2356223512ff1cb3cebdbff6.th.jpg) (http://img168.imageshack.us/my.php?image=2356223512ff1cb3cebdbff6.jpg)

(http://img515.imageshack.us/img515/5015/26479138430d46f223afbyb6.th.jpg) (http://img515.imageshack.us/my.php?image=26479138430d46f223afbyb6.jpg)

When completed our kitchen will be much bigger (at least 2x the square footage, counter space, and storage...yes the original design was that bad).  We enjoy being here.  I really like the fact that it's next to the park (one of the main reasons why we bought here).  It's nice to walk down to the pool in the morning to do laps or cool off when the it gets hot.  It was a treat to watch the 4th of July fireworks from the rooftop (albeit rainy night).  It would be nice to have a meet and greet sometime this summer with others who live in the building.

Title: Re: Pistilli Riverview East Apartments
Post by: t3knoid on July 12, 2008, 08:40:55 PM
Quote from: Handyman on July 12, 2008, 05:16:07 PM
I gotta say, I like the brick.    Real or faux?

They're real bricks...1/2 inch ones.  We had a really talented brick layer do the work.  The sheet-rock behind it was first covered to avoid moisture build-up.  A metal "chicken-wire" was then added and cemented over before the brick was laid on.  I was hesitant at first in doing the brick thinking it would give a "country" style look.  I am very happy on how it turned out.  It also gave me the opportunity to run all the speaker wires and A/V channels behind the wall.  The wall looks and feels like it's been there for ages.
Title: Re: Pistilli Riverview East Apartments

hehe...Star Wars is big in this household...Its my test DVD when I finished hooking up the A/V system  :-).  Our kitchen renovation is very dramatic compared to what it was.  We basically gutted the original (cabinets, appliances, and sink) and tore down a wall and a closet.  We kept the floor tiles (we were able to find a supplier of the original).  This saved us a good amount of money.  We designed the kitchen layout ourselves and went to Home Depot for the cabinets.  They were very helpful and knowledgeable when it came to putting together the details.

Title: Re: Pistilli Riverview East Apartments
Post by: TJT on July 15, 2008, 12:51:46 PM
Great!  Then tell me more about that brick.  I was looking at some faux stone wall treatments that are very popular now. We saw a display at the recent International Design Expo.  These are man-made materials and are either interlocking or applied with an adhesive.  The main advantage is that there is practically no mess to the room.  See this photo to get an idea of the product.  Also, if you ever eat in Luna De Juarez on Steinway (great food), you will see they used almost an overabundance of this material.  I ask because we are at a pretty final stage now and we cannot bring in buckets of wet mortar.  So I'm always interested in materials that can have the smallest impact on the rest of the apartment.  But I would definitely consider the bricks you used for the next apartment we buy. 

(http://www.culturedstone.com/photos/large/interior/architkhsint85.jpg)
Title: Re: Pistilli Riverview East Apartments
Post by: t3knoid on July 15, 2008, 08:20:48 PM
The bricks are real bricks...the same dimensions as a normal brick, just thinner..about 1/2" thick.  It's attached to the wall using mortar (first treating the wall behind it as I described previously).  It does get messy when the brick is cut...most of the cutting was done on the terrace...but we had to cut some details after the brick was up so that created a lot of dust in the living room. 

I'm not familiar with any faux stones so I can't speak about them.  It looks nice on the photo, but you can't really judge something like this based on a picture.  I really wanted something that looks and feel like a real brick wall.  I've seen faux brick walls before and wasn't happy with how they looked.  It was actually at a design show where I first saw these bricks and gave me some confidence on installing them.  I was surprised that these things actually existed.  I suggest going to the place where we bought the materials from:

Extech Industries 43-87 Vernon Blvd. Long Island City, NY 11101 718-786-2288

You might get more ideas from them...plus its fun if you like renovating  :-).

Title: Re: Pistilli Riverview East Apartments
Post by: TJT on July 16, 2008, 10:43:39 AM
It's a very interesting product, these thin bricks.  But they do seem to require a similar installation to a traditional brick wall in terms of the wall prep and the actual "brick laying" with the mortar and the whole nine yards.  I'm old enough to remember the awful faux brick of the 70s that was all the rage.  Like the expression goes, it's come a LONG way since then.  The stones like in my photo are a dead ringer for real stone when you see them in person.  And surprisingly, even to the touch.  The only real sign is if you knock on it, you will hear the hollow echo and a vinyl/plastic tone.  Our personal issue is that we spent the past couple of years doing both heavy and light renovations to every room in our apartment.  We're finally at the stage of decorating elements such as final choices in furnishings, lighting, art, etc.  So bringing in a brick layer at this point is just not happening for us.  It's easier to move!  So when we saw the guys at the trade show with this artificial product, it was interesting.  The material is made by Owens Corning (and competitors, of course).  You basically use an adhesive like Loctite in a caulking gun on the wall.  Then stack these "blocks" of stone on the wall like a puzzle.  No prep.  Just a drop cloth underneath in case of an accident.  They even cut with a heavy duty shears.  (Oh how I remember the wet saw on the terrace.  And the chalky splatter of granite dust all over our windows)  Anyway - that's it - pretty simple.  What's held us back is the concern that in ten years, it might scream 2008.  In other words, your job is real brick and looks like plain brick - it's timeless.  This fake stuff looks nice now but it could turn off a buyer down the road and that's not a good thing.  I love experimenting with unusual paint techniques and doing accent walls.  I may stick to that.  If it's not someones taste - or if we tire of it - just get a gallon of paint and go over it.
